  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_004617f3-d0aa-4cfd-a3a5-c58124d56e8d'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

  <span id="freeText4417701777128161977" style="display:none">Glen Runciter está muerto. ¿O lo están todos los demás? Lo que es seguro es que alguien ha muerto en una explosión organizada por los competidores de Runciter. De hecho, sus empleados asisten a un funeral. Pero durante el duelo comienzan a recibir mensajes descorcentantes, e incluso morbosos, de su jefe. Y el mundo a su alrededor comienza a desmoronarse de un modo que sugiere que a ellos tampoco les queda mucho tiempo.<br /><br /> Esta mordaz comedia metafísica de muerte y salvación (que podrá llevar un cómodo envase) es un tour de force de amenaza paranoica y comedia absurda, en la cual los muertos ofrecen consejos comerciales, compran su siguiente reencarnación y corren el riesgo continuo de volver a morir.</span>

<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/29134572-melissa-rininger"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1393552398i/29134572._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/29134572-melissa-rininger">Melissa Rininger</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">The short answer: The coin is a reference to Plato. Wisdom is the key to unlocking the metaphysical question of reality: "There is one true coin for w<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">The short answer: The coin is a reference to Plato. Wisdom is the key to unlocking the metaphysical question of reality: "There is one true coin for which all things ought to be exchanged, and that is wisdom." Dick constructed the world of Ubik in a Platonic system: Theory of Knowledge, Theory of Forms, and the Conception of the Tripartite Soul. By the end of the novel, Joe Chip uses Socrates method of dialectics to see the difference between empirical reality and absolute Forms. He becomes the logistikan (logic), Ella represents spirit, and Jory represents appetite. Once wisdom (logic) overcomes the body's appetites, then logic/spirit/appetite align as one and they transcend--becoming the Total Man. The coin represents their transcendence and becoming One with God/the Immanent Mind/the Immovable Creator/the Univeral Being/whatever-you-want-to-call-IT. Ubik is a cosmological explanation for Dick's schizophrenic hallucinations and visions. The coin implies the nature of reality is cyclical and the direction of movement is circular depth, not linear.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

      $('span_class_gr-hyperlink_sort_order_span_tip_481').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

<div id="review_137650908" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/137650908" />
    <a title="RandomAnthony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/721021-randomanthony"><img alt="RandomAnthony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470919631p2/721021.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/137650908?book_show_action=true">Jan 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="RandomAnthony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="RandomAnthony" href="/user/show/721021-randomanthony">RandomAnthony</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer137650908" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer436432797418474605">Phillip K Dick's <i>Ubik</i> flirts with perfection. I inhaled this novel over three days when one of my kids was sick and Christmas break was ending. I started the book on the couch during a <i> Mythbusters </i> marathon. By page fifty I wanted to shut the door and leave my kids to forage in the refrigerator for Gatorade and string cheese. And on Sunday night, when I closed the book, I felt satisfied and excited with a novel in a way that doesn't happen much. <i>Ubik</i> is fun, smart, and exhilarating. <br/><br/>Ok, let me </span>
  <span id="freeText436432797418474605" style="display:none">Phillip K Dick's <i>Ubik</i> flirts with perfection. I inhaled this novel over three days when one of my kids was sick and Christmas break was ending. I started the book on the couch during a <i> Mythbusters </i> marathon. By page fifty I wanted to shut the door and leave my kids to forage in the refrigerator for Gatorade and string cheese. And on Sunday night, when I closed the book, I felt satisfied and excited with a novel in a way that doesn't happen much. <i>Ubik</i> is fun, smart, and exhilarating. <br /><br />Ok, let me take a shot at the plot summary. Joe Chip works for a team that shields organizations and the general public from illegal super-psychological activity like, for example, the unethical use of precognition. I think. Anyway, Mr. Chip is down and out, almost too broke to pay the nickel necessary to operate his apartment door. He is charged by his employer (and his employer's wife, currently in “half-life”, a finite state in which the dead and living can interact) with leading a team to Luna in search of the criminals of whom they lost track. From there <i>Ubik</i> takes off into territory defying summarization. I'd need a chart to track all the turns and potentialities. The novel addresses Chip's attempt to separate multiple realities and discern exactly who he is, where he is, and when he is. Somewhere in there Dick batters around the I-Ching and Plato's form philosophy. <i>Ubik</i>'s genius emerges in Dick's obsessive attention to detail. He's a remarkably disciplined writer for a guy who sounds completely messed up (more on his biography in a second). The novel never goes dry; Dick balances the esoteric, theoretical analysis with an urgent storyline. Joe Chip's inner monologue, his attempts to piece together the myriad of clues pointing to the establishment and resolution of his questions, is paranoid, desperate, and brilliant. <i>Ubik</i>, and PKD's work in general, is a significant element of the genre's template. This is the third PKD novel I've read, and although I don't want to snap them up in a rush, I'll hit more this year.<br /><br />Oh, I should mention that I read the Library of America edition of this novel. The LOA edition (you know, those heavy black books with the nifty attached bookmark) includes three other novels, notes from Jonathan Lethem, and a detailed author timeline/biography. Holy hell, PDK lived a fucked-up life, between social anxiety, industrial strength drug use, and multiple stints in psychiatric care. That said, I love the fact this novel was published in 1969. Put <i>Ubik</i> in your summer of love pipe and smoke it, hippies. <br /><br />I don't want to become a star-whore. Over the last year I've assigned four books five stars. Maybe I'm getting soft. The little note over the fifth star, however, reads “It was amazing”, and those three words fit <i>Ubik</i>, so I'm sticking with the fifth star. This novel is the poster child for the difference between workmanlike genre fiction (nothing wrong with that) and the kind that makes you want to jump and down with your hands in the air like you're a twelve year old at his first rock concert. I want to hang its poster over my bed and blow kisses to <i>Ubik</i> before I fall asleep.</span>
  <a data-text-id="436432797418474605" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2319614202" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2319614202" />
    <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus"><img alt="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588320656p2/78485297.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2319614202?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus">Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus?shelf=dick-philip-k">dick-philip-k</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus?shelf=science-fiction-old">science-fiction-old</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2319614202" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4465322112014841583">The idea of the one wonder-substance, superdrug, holy grail, dietary supplement,… to rule or enhance them all is an old one, but it needed Dicks´tendency to integrate mental illness, illusion, conspiracy, different realities and madness in the mix to make it a new one. So what to think about this?<br/><br/>I like this one more than „Do androids dream of electric sheep.“, because the plot is so dense, the ideas wrapped around it ingeniously and probably because Dick had so many drug experiences that writin</span>
  <span id="freeText4465322112014841583" style="display:none">The idea of the one wonder-substance, superdrug, holy grail, dietary supplement,… to rule or enhance them all is an old one, but it needed Dicks´tendency to integrate mental illness, illusion, conspiracy, different realities and madness in the mix to make it a new one. So what to think about this?<br /><br />I like this one more than „Do androids dream of electric sheep.“, because the plot is so dense, the ideas wrapped around it ingeniously and probably because Dick had so many drug experiences that writing about a topic like that was simply his thing.<br /><br />The interpretations could go in all directions, biochemical, religious, pharmaceutic, transcendental, whatever one prefers and I will choose the economic one by saying that we are already running on so many wonder substances like the marvelous caffeine and that tech will someday bring us something close to a real ubik. Probably including some time travel, Psi and mind uploading to that Dick´s vision can become reality.<br /><br />I added, or at this moment more precisely , will add this part to my review of „Do Androids dream of electric sheep“ too, because it´s appropriate and fitting for both novels. <br /><br />The writing style is typical, one red line, no real subplots, the ending is quite kind of unsatisfying (looking at you, Man in the high castle.), it often gets confusing and it´s difficult to differentiate if it´s ingenuity or the authors' illumination. All of that are reasons why Dick is more controversial and not so universally acclaimed a grandmaster of Sci-Fi and I am more on the side of his critics. If one looks at the worldbuilding and complexity of all the other behemoths, Dick seems average, with the only hobbyhorse of dealing with consciousness, reality and the mentioned topics and some novels feel as if he just wrote them for the money (he needed) without real intrinsic motivation. I would call him, and I hardly ever do that because it is not nice, overrated. In this regard, he is more like the Nobel price, pseudo-intellectual, overhyped, higher literature stuff and less like pure, true, entertaining fiction. To write not understandable and confusing to seem deep and arcane is much easier than to write entertaining, suspenseful and yes, true, stereotypical following the rules of the genre. But that´s one of the key elements of why we love certain genres and tinkering around with conventions while writing 60 pages a day under the influence of LSD and amphetamines brings him in the corner of Kerouac and consorts and „first thought best thought.“<br /><br />Dicks´ novels don´t feel coherent, there are no satisfying resolutions, just more and more mysteries and open questions and nothing gets answered and much feels unfinished. It's no bad writing, I just wouldn´t highly recommend it, because it are no fun reads and if Dick would have been a bit soberer and invested more time in developing satisfying, believable plots, that could have been great. What annoys me the most are the great moments and ideas that are followed by unanswered questions, unreliable protagonist behavior or losing the overview of what is happening.<br /><br />A direct comparison with other grandmasters of Sci-Fi and what they have revolutionized shows the flaws even clearer. Heinlein with amazing military science fiction, Asimovs´robots and some of the first space opera, Clarkes unbelievable language and subtility, Pohl with his worldbuilding, Gibson with Cyberpunk, not to name all the newer authors, and Stanislaw Lem. Especially he would have deserved the same and more attention and appreciation as Dick and should be named in a row with Asimov, Clarke, and Heinlein because he wrote revolutionary brilliant at Clarkes´ level and was really funny in other novels and short stories, highly recommended literature, he is unique. All those authors were able to write entertaining, unique, tropeforming, philosophical, and with metaplots that come all together to a satisfying and logical ending, something Dick was incapable of, because he didn´t construct a universe, just fragments not fitting together and of extremely varying quality.<br /><br />Of course, it may be a question of personal taste and preference, but I have read so much great Sci-Fi, hundreds of novels, that it feels inappropriate to name him in a line with those works and I felt really unsatisfied after having read any of his novels that are all closer to psychological mindf*** mystery whodunnit whatever crossover hybrid than to real Sci-Fi and with less real genre-typical elements in them. All the giants were true intellectuals and able to endlessly talk about any tiny detail or their works and its meaning and sense and it would interest me if Dick would have been able to give answers to complex questions about his work.<br /><br />Tropes show how literature is conceptualized and created and which mixture of elements makes works and genres unique: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.php/Creator/PhilipKDick">https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.ph...</a><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.php/Literature/Ubik">https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.ph...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="4465322112014841583" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer431816312" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12847341655450075090">I began reading some of Philip K. Dick’s short stories and quickly became hooked. His style and imagination have left an indelible mark on science fiction since and his influence is unmistakable. His novels are genius, and Ubik may be the best one I have read yet. <br/><br/>Telling an inventive sci-fi tale that is entertaining on its surface, this is also a theological metaphor that keeps the reader thinking and trying to figure out what in the world PKD is getting at. His brilliance is compelling and his</span>
  <span id="freeText12847341655450075090" style="display:none">I began reading some of Philip K. Dick’s short stories and quickly became hooked. His style and imagination have left an indelible mark on science fiction since and his influence is unmistakable. His novels are genius, and Ubik may be the best one I have read yet. <br><br>Telling an inventive sci-fi tale that is entertaining on its surface, this is also a theological metaphor that keeps the reader thinking and trying to figure out what in the world PKD is getting at. His brilliance is compelling and his forays into a more absurd fiction only heighten his return to substantial narrative, but all is held together by his unmistakable voice. <br><br>Ubik explores many of his usual themes like alienation, isolation, theological mystery and a disconnection with advancing technology. PKD is one of the more cerebral of the genre’s authors and if Heinlein, Asimov and Clarke are the “Big Three” of hard science fiction I would submit that Dick, Bradbury and Le Guin are the masters of the soft science fiction side of the house. <br><br>For a PKD fan, Ubik is a must read, but it is a fine book all by itself and would be a good introduction into his world.<br><br>** 2019 addendum - it is a testament to great literature that a reader recalls the work years later and this is a book about which I frequently think. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1485782362i/21846324.jpg" width="254" height="306" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="12847341655450075090" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=2013">2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer183083423" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15102419306501295253"><i>“I am Ubik. Before the universe was, I am.</i> <br/>Philip K. Dick, Ubik<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1450237820i/17399084._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='225' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><i>I made the suns. I made the worlds. I created the lives and the places they inhabit; I move them here, I put them there. They go as I say, then do as I tell them. I am the word and my name is never spoken, the name which no one knows. I am called Ubik, but that is not my name. I am. I shall always be.”</i> <br/>― Philip K. Dick, Ubik<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1450237820i/17399085.jpg' width='400' height='225' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><b>Friends, this wild review is 100% PKD approved. Ubik the review is only seconds away! Ubik the review is easy-t</b></span>
  <span id="freeText15102419306501295253" style="display:none"><i>“I am Ubik. Before the universe was, I am.</i> <br>Philip K. Dick, Ubik<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1450237820i/17399084._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="225" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>I made the suns. I made the worlds. I created the lives and the places they inhabit; I move them here, I put them there. They go as I say, then do as I tell them. I am the word and my name is never spoken, the name which no one knows. I am called Ubik, but that is not my name. I am. I shall always be.”</i> <br>― Philip K. Dick, Ubik<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1450237820i/17399085.jpg" width="400" height="225" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Friends, this wild review is 100% PKD approved. Ubik the review is only seconds away! Ubik the review is easy-to-read, easy-to-understand, nonflammable, and avoids directly mentioning those aspects of your existence that might make you squirm. Safe when read as directed. Avoid prolonged viewing. Beware grammatical and typographical errors.</b><br>____________________________<br><br>Dick, like Pynchon, has a THING for entropy and he perfected this theme in <i>Ubik</i>. While not a direct part of Dick's Gnostic God trilogy (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/216377.VALIS__VALIS_Trilogy___1_" title="VALIS (VALIS Trilogy, #1) by Philip K. Dick" rel="nofollow noopener">VALIS</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/216398.The_Divine_Invasion" title="The Divine Invasion by Philip K. Dick" rel="nofollow noopener">The Divine Invasion</a>, &amp; <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/106585.The_Transmigration_of_Timothy_Archer" title="The Transmigration of Timothy Archer by Philip K. Dick" rel="nofollow noopener">The Transmigration of Timothy Archer</a>), Ubik still manages to be one of Dick's minor God novels (if the Valis trilogy:Dick's trinity :: Ubik:Dick's Demiurge). Dick seems more than willing to bend entire universes to create a world where he can ask some really BIG questions in ways that give you two more levels of uncertainty. What? WHAT? <br><br>My first introduction to Dick was age 19. In a SLC airport I bought a copy of Valis (cover looked cool) and figured it would be a fun book to read on a plane. Hours later the plane landed and 20+ years later, I don't think the Earth I landed on was the same as the one the plane left prior to me cracking open Valis. Every time I read another of PKD's novels it is the exact same thing. Something breaks. Time freaks out or at least flips a bit. Something in my brain gets frozen, something else in my head gets lost, and a memory gets replaced. Each Dick novel should come with its own Ubik-type of warning: <b>this novel will alter your reality, even when read as directed.</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="15102419306501295253" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2096454454" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2096454454" />
    <a title="Glenn Russell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell"><img alt="Glenn Russell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505195595p2/23385697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2096454454?book_show_action=true">Sep 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glenn Russell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glenn Russell" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell">Glenn Russell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/13051265-ubik">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2096454454" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14066064863883924533"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1504387030i/23752044._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>“He felt all at once like an ineffectual moth, fluttering at the windowpane of reality, dimly seeing it from outside.”<br/>― Philip K. Dick, <i>Ubik</i><br/><br/>Over-the-top zany madness, Philip K. Dick’s 1969 acclaimed work of science fiction opens in the year 1992, by which time humanity has colonized the Moon aka Luna and individuals having various psychic powers are commonplace, so much so some companies hire men and women (called “telepaths” or “precogs”) based on their power to predict the future and other com</span>
  <span id="freeText14066064863883924533" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1504387030i/23752044._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>“He felt all at once like an ineffectual moth, fluttering at the windowpane of reality, dimly seeing it from outside.”<br>― Philip K. Dick, <i>Ubik</i><br><br>Over-the-top zany madness, Philip K. Dick’s 1969 acclaimed work of science fiction opens in the year 1992, by which time humanity has colonized the Moon aka Luna and individuals having various psychic powers are commonplace, so much so some companies hire men and women (called “telepaths” or “precogs”) based on their power to predict the future and other companies hire individuals (called inertials) who have the psychic clout to block the future-telling capacities of those telepaths and precogs. <br><br>If all this sounds wild, you are absolutely right – novel as sheer craziness, a book defying any straightforward synopsis. To share a glimpse into the world of Ubik, here's a round of zaps from PKD's outlandish fictional zippy zap gun: <br><br>Glen Runciter – Crusty, lovable head of Runciter Associates, a “prudence organization” which employs inertials to counter evildoing telepaths and precogs who go about snooping into other people’s stream-of-consciousness in order to predict the future. Glen is a man of integrity, forever attempting to uphold individual freedom and dignity, the kind of guy you would always want around even if he were murdered. Yes, that’s what I said – to better understand the dynamics of the novel’s unique cycle of life and death, please read on. <br><br>Ella Runciter – Glen’s deceased wife kept in a form of cryonic suspension, a state of "half-life” enabling the dearly departed a degree of awareness sufficient to communicate with their loved ones left behind and other half-lifers. Ella is kept at the Beloved Brethren Moratorium in Zurich since the Swiss have developed a superior method to effectively extend life beyond the grave. Considering the Swiss mastery in manufacturing timepieces, their superiority in cryonic technology makes perfect sense. Ah, leave it to the Swiss! <br><br>Joe Chip - Debt-ridden Runciter Assocation technician loyal to Glen, Ella, the Association, truth and justice. An All-American Joe, you might say and you gotta love the name Chip as in potato chip or chocolate chip. As it turns out, Joe takes center stage as main character when he is propelled into the role of an Indiana Jones-style American hero and leader in a unique time travel adventure that could only be concocted from the fertile psychedelic imagination of the incomparable PKD. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1506002081i/23977952._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Joe Chip is the prototypical All-American Joe <br><br>Inertials – Don, Al, Wendy are among Glen Runciter’s top inertials chosen for a special mission to Luna. If they only knew the challenges they will be forced to confront once catastrophe hits - time warps enough to confuse, blur, muddle and cloud the most perceptive minds. What those inertials really need is leadership and guidance from none other than down-to-earth Joe Chip.<br><br>Pat Conley – An enigmatic, cagey dark beauty with the unique psychic ability to undo events by changing the past. Having such a unique ability, Glen Runciter decides to include Pat in the critically important mission to Luna. As events transpire, Pat might even be judged a femme fatale along the lines of Phyllis from James M. Cain’s <i>Double Indemnity</i> in the sense that she not only deals with death and dying, she loves death and dying in all its grim, deadly detail. Just the kind of gal you want along when your group starts dying off one at a time in mysterious ways. <br><br>Beyond Pay Toilets - The author’s futuristic society includes speaking doors, speaking refrigerators and speaking coffee machines that demand money to be used – one aspect of future technology we can only hope never becomes a reality. I wonder if PKD’s personal experience with the appearance of pay toilet back in 1960s America prompted him to include these obnoxious speaking objects requiring money to operate. <br><br>Boom! - From the moment of the explosion on Luna, the group begins to experience strange shifts in reality and time, including a number of chapters in their adventure covering the United States back in 1939. One of the more humorous parts has Joe Chip flying in one of those newly invented two-person single prop airplanes from New York to Des Moines, Iowa. Wow! Now that's a dedicated hero! <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1506003324i/23978055._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Gnostic Cosmology – The further and deeper Joe and the group progresses in their odyssey, the more they become aware that they are living in a universe where the forces of light battles the forces of darkness. But then the question arises: Who or what is the ultimate source of light on one hand and darkness on the other? Enough PKD unexpected twists to keep any fan of science fiction or speculate fiction going right up til the last page. <br><br>UBIK – “Perk up pouting household surfaces with new miracle Ubik, the easy-to-apply, extra-shiny, nonstick plastic coating. Entirely harmless if used as directed. Saves endless scrubbing, glides right out of the kitchen!” Oh, yes, short advertisements for Ubik like this one precede every chapter. And please keep in mind that any cosmology, even a dualistic cosmology, might be held together by a unifying underlying metaphysical principle. What is meant by this quizzical statement? You will have to read Ubik for yourself to find out – proceed with caution and take only as directed. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1506778538i/24051919._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1505088036i/23880968._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>“Wake up to a hearty, lip-smacking bowlful of nutritious, nourishing Ubik toasted flakes, the adult cereal that’s more crunchy, more tasty, more ummmish. Ubik breakfast cereal, the whole-bowl taste treat!”<br>― Philip K. Dick, <i>Ubik</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="14066064863883924533" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_580647608" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/580647608" />
    <a title="Cecily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily"><img alt="Cecily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480285828p2/1199525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/580647608?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cecily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cecily" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily">Cecily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780575079212" href="/book/show/895473.Ubik">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=usa-and-canada">usa-and-canada</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=scifi-future-speculative-fict">scifi-future-speculative-fict</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=humour">humour</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer580647608" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3825977421395190700">A clever, original and often very funny sci-fi story. It is about psychic power battles, the nature of death, alternative reality and changing the past. Or not.<br/><br/><b>FUN, FUN, FUN - the clothes</b><br/>It was published in 1969 and starts off in a sufficiently plausible but amusingly implausible 1992. In particular, the clothes take the flamboyance of the late '60s to extraordinary heights, for no obvious reason, other than fun. On the second page, we meet a man wearing "a tabby-fur blazer and pointed yellow sh</span>
  <span id="freeText3825977421395190700" style="display:none">A clever, original and often very funny sci-fi story. It is about psychic power battles, the nature of death, alternative reality and changing the past. Or not.<br /><br /><b>FUN, FUN, FUN - the clothes</b><br />It was published in 1969 and starts off in a sufficiently plausible but amusingly implausible 1992. In particular, the clothes take the flamboyance of the late '60s to extraordinary heights, for no obvious reason, other than fun. On the second page, we meet a man wearing "a tabby-fur blazer and pointed yellow shoes", which is fair enough, but only three pages later, an elderly man wears "a varicolored... suit, knit cummerbund and dip dyed cheesecloth cravat". After that, you're on the lookout for them, so here are more:<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />* "gay pin-stripe clown-style pajamas"<br />* "a sporty maroon wrapper, twinkle-toes turned-up shoes and a felt cap with a tassel"<br />* "electric yellow cummerbund, petal skirt, knee-hugging hose and military-styled visored cap" plus gauntlets. And that's a man.<br />* "a cowboy hat, black lace mantilla and Bermuda shorts"<br />* "wrapped in a superior and cynical cloud of pride"<br />* "floral mumu and spandex bloomers"<br />* "natty birk-bark pantaloons, hemp rope belt, peek-a-boo see-through top and train-engineer's tall hat" (a man, as most of these are)<br />* "hip-hugging gold lamé trousers, yet somehow created a stylish effect. Perhaps the egg-sized buttons of his kelp-green mitty blouse helped... he exuded a dignity"!<br />* "a shift dress the color of a baboon's ass" (a man)<br />* "fuchsia pedal-pushers, pink yak fur slippers, a snakeskin sleeveless blouse and a ribbon in his waist-length, dyed white hair"<br />* "the elastic band which - fashionably - compressed her breasts... had elegant embossed fleur-de-lys"<br />* "tweed toga, loafers, crimson sash and purple airplane-propeller hat"<br />* "green felt knickers, gray golf socks, badger-hide open-midriff blouse and imitation patent-leather pumps"<br />* A girl wearing jeans, a canvas work shirt and muddy boots is told she's "dressed oddly"!<br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br />You have to wonder what might have prompted such wild flights of fancy. ;)<br /><br />Another distinctive feature is that every chapter is prefaced with a short advert for Ubik, and each one demonstrates a different and amazing use for the wonder product. Each ends with a slightly worrying caveat about only being safe if used exactly as directed. Its enormous range of uses remind me of <b>Flanders and Swann's Wompom</b>. They sing it <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ivf4w3r-_3c" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>, or read the lyrics <a href="http://www.elyrics.net/read/f/flanders-&amp;-swann-lyrics/the-wompom-lyrics.html" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>. I've also reviewed their songbook <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1983047360" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.<br /><br />Twice, characters say "so it goes", which I assumed was a nod to the famous catchphrase of Vonnegut's Slaughterhouse Five (see my review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/129649718" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>), but both were published in the same year, so I guess it's just a coincidence. There is also mention of a dead parrot, but that can't be a nod to Monty Python as their famous sketch also dates from... 1969! Spooky, eh? Maybe PKD really could glimpse the future?!<br /><br />Runciter's space transporter is called Pratfall II. <br /><br /><b>PLOT</b><br />Anyway, the crux of the story is the constant battle between people with psychic powers (such as precognition and telepathy) and "prudence" organisations that supply "inertials" who block such powers (often by using such powers themselves). Who are the goodies and who the baddies in such a setup: "A policeman guarding human privacy" or "trying to turn the clock back"?<br /><br />Glen Runciter is the larger than life figure who heads one such prudence organisation, and Joe Chip is his right hand man. Pat is a new recruit who can change the past in such a way that people don't even know it. She and Joe may or may not have a thing for each other.<br /><br />They and eleven of their best go to Luna for a rather mysterious job. After that, things turn strange: perceptions of reality shift, and time seems to slip back as well. Some objects age, some change, but not everyone's experience is the same. Are they going back in time, is the past receding, or are they in some other reality? The only shame is that from this point on, the clothes are less mad.<br /><br />Joe is the principal character trying to work out what is going on, how to survive and so on. It's hard to explain further without spoilers.<br /><br /><b>THE FUTURE</b><br />Dick's 1992 is very commercialised: you have to pay for almost everything, though it's mostly coin-operated - even one's own front door! When someone couldn't find a coin and tried to dismantle his own lock, it threatened to sue him!<br /><br />But on Luna, "All our medical care... is free. But the burden of proof that he is genuinely ill rests on the shoulders of the alleged patient." I hope no UK politicians think of that as a way to "save" the NHS whilst also saving money. (They'll love the "alleged".)<br /><br />Dick doesn't foresee mobile internet etc (who did?), but the pape machine is rather like a printer connected to the internet.<br /><br /><b>HALF-LIFE</b><br />Runciter's wife, Ella is at a moratorium, in cold-pac half-life. She died, or near enough, but is in cold storage which provides a sort of life-extension. Most of the time she's unconscious, but she can occasionally be contacted; how many times, over how many years depends on lots of factors around the death and the freezing. <br /><br />The moratorium and its inhabitants are significant plot elements, but are also used to explore the fuzzy boundary between life and death. Runciter consults with Ella, but how is this different from using a medium to consult the properly dead? Those in half-live can sometimes communicate with each other, "wandering through one another's mind gives those in half-life the only -", but they can't initiate contact with those outside. "'She exists... she merely can't contact you.' Runciter said 'A metaphysical difference which means nothing to me.'" <br /><br /><b>QUOTES</b><br />* "Herbert felt the weight of the hand, its persuading vigor". Runciter's hand (and vigor).<br />* "Nothing touched his mind... He chuckled, but it had an abstract quality... his voice always boomed, but inside he did not notice anyone, did not care; it was his body which smiled, nodded and shook hands." (Runciter again.)<br />* A messy apartment "radiated the specter of debris and clutter".<br />* "On his face, a feral, hateful expression formed, giving him the expression of a psychotic squirrel."<br />* "His voice had a squeaky, penetrating, castrato quality to it, an unpleasant noise that one might expect to hear... from a hive of metal bees."</span>
  <a data-text-id="3825977421395190700" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1731323501" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1731323501" />
    <a title="Kevin Ansbro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43579369-kevin-ansbro"><img alt="Kevin Ansbro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506848923p2/43579369.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1731323501?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kevin Ansbro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kevin Ansbro" href="/user/show/43579369-kevin-ansbro">Kevin Ansbro</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780575079212" href="/book/show/895473.Ubik">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Readers who like their sci fi laced  with deadpan humour.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43579369-kevin-ansbro?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43579369-kevin-ansbro?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43579369-kevin-ansbro?shelf=recommended-by-cecily">recommended-by-cecily</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43579369-kevin-ansbro?shelf=funny-as-f">funny-as-f</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43579369-kevin-ansbro?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43579369-kevin-ansbro?shelf=escapism">escapism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43579369-kevin-ansbro?shelf=years-ahead-of-its-time">years-ahead-of-its-time</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43579369-kevin-ansbro?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43579369-kevin-ansbro?shelf=tongue-in-cheek">tongue-in-cheek</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1731323501" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17623726895147165498">.<br/>.<br/><b>
  <i>"He felt all at once like an ineffectual moth, fluttering at the windowpane of reality, dimly seeing it from the outside." </i>
</b><br/>-Philip K Dick<br/><br/><b>
  <i>"A fool and his poscreds are soon parted."</i>
</b><br/>-Kevin Ansbro<br/><br/>Please allow me to preface my review by stating that sci-fi is not normally my <i>thang</i>. Aside from Asimov, when I was a teenager, I've preferred to watch it, and write it, rather than read it. In fact, were it not for Obi-Wan Cecily's recommendation, I might have erroneously imagined Philip K Dick t</span>
  <span id="freeText17623726895147165498" style="display:none">.<br />.<br /><b>
  <i>"He felt all at once like an ineffectual moth, fluttering at the windowpane of reality, dimly seeing it from the outside." </i>
</b><br />-Philip K Dick<br /><br /><b>
  <i>"A fool and his poscreds are soon parted."</i>
</b><br />-Kevin Ansbro<br /><br />Please allow me to preface my review by stating that sci-fi is not normally my <i>thang</i>. Aside from Asimov, when I was a teenager, I've preferred to watch it, and write it, rather than read it. In fact, were it not for Obi-Wan Cecily's recommendation, I might have erroneously imagined Philip K Dick to have been a 1970s' porn star!<br /><br />Well, I'm relieved and pleased to report that this pre-cyberpunk gigglefest was an absolute joy to behold!<br /><br />Written in the late 1960s, <i>Ubik</i> is set in the 'future' of 1992, a future we've overstepped without one sniff of dystopia.<br />From way back then, Dick presents us with an analogue dreamworld that we can still enjoy in a digital age. <br />Despite mention of videotape and typewriters, it still feels futuristic.<br /><br />So how to explain this quizzical space oddity?<br />I would bill it as a <i>Truman Show-Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy-Barbarella</i>-type of sci-fi, dressed in a groovy wardrobe of clothes that would make even Austin Powers seem sartorially conservative.<br /><br />I confess to having had concerns at the start. The first few chapters had me wondering if I should permanently abscond from Goodreads altogether, for fear of offending friend-fans of this book.<br />But then joyously it breaks into a canter and I was suddenly in sync with the timbre of its prose.<br /><br />The anti-hero of the piece is sullen Joe Chip, a chap who is worn down by a futureworld of talking appliances and argumentative doors (frustrating for him, but completely hilarious for us).<br />Joe falls under the spell of a Lara Croft-esque mind control babe, Patricia Conley. <br />They, and their Magnificent Eleven parapsychological team of individuals are conscripted for an audacious project.<br /><br />Without giving anything away, what ensues is a <i>Memento</i>-style mindfrig that will have you second-guessing <i>everything</i>.<br /><br />The author writes in an idiosyncratically surreal way: he invents words and (deliberately?) misapplies adjectives to achieve an <i>avant-garde</i> effect. In addition, Dick uses near-synonyms of better-suited verbs in his bid to create additional quirkiness.<br /><br />There is of course a cautionary message: he has presciently foreseen a future where automation hijacks our civilisation. Think about it; one minute we're scanning our own blasted shopping at crummy self-service checkouts, and the next thing you know we'll be held to ransom under the tyranny of obdurate machines and talking refrigerators!<br /><br />I am delighted to join the fan base of this capricious nonsense. It is altogether bizarre, thought-provoking, visionary and hugely funny.<br /><i>Ubik</i> is the work of a mad genius - and it has immediately gatecrashed my favourites list.<br />The ending is as enigmatic as its beginning and is open to any number of interpretations.<br />Here, for sure, the journey is get-down-boogiewoogie-fabulous. The final destination is partly left to our own imagination.<br /><br />I owe a debt of thanks to Cecily, the Earth-based precog, who of course already knew that I would enjoy this read!<br />And as a special treat to myself, I'm off to get me some gold lamé trousers, a pair of Spandex bloomers, some pink yakfur slippers and I'm hitting the town!<br />Yeah baby, yeah!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="17623726895147165498" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2454425234" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2454425234" />
    <a title="Tara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70459127-tara"><img alt="Tara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592666421p2/70459127.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2454425234?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tara" href="/user/show/70459127-tara">Tara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780547572291" href="/book/show/12346742-ubik">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2454425234" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16787973570493024765"><blockquote>
  <i>“He felt all at once like an ineffectual moth, fluttering at the windowpane of reality, dimly seeing it from outside.”</i>
</blockquote><br/><i>Ubik</i> is a fun, fascinating, and often surprisingly philosophical look at the nature of reality and the role of our perception thereof. PKD also delves masterfully, cleverly, and even quite exuberantly, into some of his other favorite food for thought, which in this case includes entropy, alienation, and the question of (in)sanity, to name but a few. All the while, the story p</span>
  <span id="freeText16787973570493024765" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>“He felt all at once like an ineffectual moth, fluttering at the windowpane of reality, dimly seeing it from outside.”</i>
</blockquote><br><i>Ubik</i> is a fun, fascinating, and often surprisingly philosophical look at the nature of reality and the role of our perception thereof. PKD also delves masterfully, cleverly, and even quite exuberantly, into some of his other favorite food for thought, which in this case includes entropy, alienation, and the question of (in)sanity, to name but a few. All the while, the story playfully and persistently messes with your mind in a most enjoyable way. You stray down strange, twisted corridors and arrive in ever stranger locales. Or is it merely that your own perception has changed, grown subtly yet indelibly distorted, somehow become increasingly warped and askew? One is never completely certain, and therein lies much of the fun. And even though the book contains a great deal of darkness and doubt, there is also quite a bit of offbeat, absurdist humor to complement (and at times even enhance) its frequently eerie, uncanny tone. <br><br>I won’t discuss any plot details here. I went into it knowing absolutely nothing about the storyline, and found it highly satisfying to figure it out for myself as I wandered slowly yet ever more curiously through this elaborately constructed labyrinthine house of mirrors. I wouldn’t want to deny anyone that experience for themselves, and sometimes that carefully hidden spoiler is just begging to be clicked, isn’t it? So instead, I’ll leave you with this rather apt PKD quote I stumbled across on the Internet. It touches on one of the questions explored so buoyantly yet deftly in this wonderfully bizarre little book:<br><blockquote>
  <i>“Maybe each human being lives in a unique world, a private world different from those inhabited and experienced by all other humans. . . If reality differs from person to person, can we speak of reality singular, or shouldn't we really be talking about plural realities? And if there are plural realities, are some more true (more real) than others? What about the world of a schizophrenic? Maybe it's as real as our world. Maybe we cannot say that we are in touch with reality and he is not, but should instead say, His reality is so different from ours that he can't explain his to us, and we can't explain ours to him. The problem, then, is that if subjective worlds are experienced too differently, there occurs a breakdown in communication ... and there is the real illness.”</i>
</blockquote><br><br>If you ever experience the sneaking suspicion that there are multiple realities in play, if you’re unable to shake that pesky feeling of existential dread and anxiety, or aren’t totally certain whether or not reality is all in your head (and where exactly <i>is</i> your head, come to think of it?), you simply <i>must</i> give Ubik a try! Read this book for more on how that handy spray can save the day! <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1531913884i/25991416._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="16787973570493024765" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_169886694" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/169886694" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/169886694?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=top-20">top-20</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=sf-top-20">sf-top-20</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer169886694" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9728090089130304247"><i>“Herr Schoenheit von Vogelsang; sorry to break into your meditation, but a customer wishes you to assist in revving up his relative.”</i><br/><br/>Haha! I don’t know if PKD intended the above dialogue to be humorous but it is so bizarre and PKD-esque it made me chuckle. There is often a weird stiltedness to his dialogue that I find oddly charming. <br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1555422452i/27371390._SY540_.jpg' width='500' height='707' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>I last read <i>Ubik</i> in 2012 (seven years ago as of today) I remember thinking “this is it, this is my favorite PKD”. Before this current reread I can barely remember a</span>
  <span id="freeText9728090089130304247" style="display:none"><i>“Herr Schoenheit von Vogelsang; sorry to break into your meditation, but a customer wishes you to assist in revving up his relative.”</i><br><br>Haha! I don’t know if PKD intended the above dialogue to be humorous but it is so bizarre and PKD-esque it made me chuckle. There is often a weird stiltedness to his dialogue that I find oddly charming. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1555422452i/27371390._SY540_.jpg" width="500" height="707" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>I last read <i>Ubik</i> in 2012 (seven years ago as of today) I remember thinking “this is it, this is my favorite PKD”. Before this current reread I can barely remember anything about the plot, only a vague image that of a spray can of <i>Ubik</i>, but what is it? <i>Ubik</i> is set in the (then) future of 1992 where psi abilities are a given fact and are often used for industrial espionage, invasion of privacy and other psychic intrusions. The situation is like computer virus and anti-virus, so there are businesses that exist purely to counter these psychic intrusions. Somewhat related to psi abilities is the “half-life” business run by “moratoriums” where, for a fee, you can store your dearly departed and they facilitate a communication channel between you and your (mostly) dead loved ones. The protagonist, Joe Chip, works for Runciter Associates, a “prudence organization” that offers anti-psi intrusion services. On an assignment to the lunar colony Joe, his boss Glen Runciter, and his team are attacked by a bomb which killed Runciter. Worse still since the bombing reality begins to bend out of shape, items begin to regress back into their dated versions until Joe finds himself in 1939.<br><br>Excellent Time magazine book reviewer and author <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/142270.Lev_Grossman" title="Lev Grossman" rel="nofollow noopener"> Lev Grossman</a> described <i>Ubik</i> as <i>“a deeply unsettling existential horror story, a nightmare you'll never be sure you've woken up from”</i>. This surprised me a little, I don’t find that <i>Ubik</i> has a horror tone and it is not at all unsettling, but wacky and very entertaining, the tone is often quite humorous; especially the <i>Ubik</i> ad at the beginning of each chapter, always as a different product from the preceding chapter.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1555422686i/27371407._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Time and time again PKD pulled the rug from under this reader, just when I thought I knew what is going on, PKD pulled another fast one and I landed on my butt. For me, his depiction of an unreliable and unstable reality is what PKD does better than anybody else. The eventual reveal of what <i>Ubik</i> blew my mind, but even then PKD has one more rug to pull.<br><br>If you have not read PKD before I highly recommend <i>Ubik</i> as the gateway into his wonderfully weird fiction. I kind of envy you.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1555422452i/27371389.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Quotes:</b><br><i>“Perhaps your definition of your self-system lacks authentic boundaries. You’ve erected a precarious structure of personality on unconscious factors over which you have no control. That’s why you feel threatened by me.”<br><br>“He therefore vigorously strode to the apt door, turned the knob and pulled on the release bolt. The door refused to open. It said, “Five cents, please.”<br><br>“Ray Hollis, whose psionically talented personnel are the object of inertial nullification and hence the target of the prudence”<br><br>“The past is latent, is submerged, but still there, capable of rising to the surface once the later imprinting unfortunately--and against ordinary experience--vanished. The man contains--not the boy--but earlier men, he thought. History began a long time ago.”</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="9728090089130304247" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2545798610" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2545798610" />
    <a title="Warwick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53165-warwick"><img alt="Warwick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510406793p2/53165.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2545798610?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Warwick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Warwick" href="/user/show/53165-warwick">Warwick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/13051265-ubik">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=united-states">united-states</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=dont-own">dont-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=read-in-other">read-in-other</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=iowa">iowa</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2545798610" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11730029864365927779">What I want more than anything right now is for some fashion designer or talented artist to do a series of illustrations of the clothing in this book. When it comes to ludicrous future fashions, <i>Ubik</i> is the Ur-text. Among the outfits described herein are the following racy numbers:<br/><br/><blockquote>• green felt knickers, gray golf socks, badger-hide open-midriff blouse and imitation patent-leather pumps [Al Hammond]<br/><br/>• a Continental outfit: tweed toga, loafers, crimson sash and a purple airplane-propellor beanie [H</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText11730029864365927779" style="display:none">What I want more than anything right now is for some fashion designer or talented artist to do a series of illustrations of the clothing in this book. When it comes to ludicrous future fashions, <i>Ubik</i> is the Ur-text. Among the outfits described herein are the following racy numbers:<br /><br /><blockquote>• green felt knickers, gray golf socks, badger-hide open-midriff blouse and imitation patent-leather pumps [Al Hammond]<br /><br />• a Continental outfit: tweed toga, loafers, crimson sash and a purple airplane-propellor beanie [Herbert Schoenheit von Vogelsang]<br /><br />• his customary natty birch-bark pantaloons, hemp-rope belt, peekaboo see-through top and train-engineer's tall hat [GG Ashwood]<br /><br />• his usual mohair poncho, apricot-colored felt hat, argyle ski socks and carpet slippers [GG Ashwood]<br /><br />• ersatz vicuna trousers and a gray sweatshirt on which had been printed a now faded full-face portrait of Bertrand Lord Russell [Tippy Jackson]<br /><br />• a polyester dirndl, his long hair in a snood, cowboy chaps with simulated silver stars. And sandals. [Don Denny]<br /><br />• a sporty maroon wrapper, twinkle-toes turned-up shoes and a felt cap with a tassel [Joe Chip]<br /><br />• his electric-yellow cummerbund, petal skirt, knee-hugging hose and military-style visored cap [standard office worker]</blockquote><br /><br />I mean…I can't even picture half of those in my head. Hence the desperate need for a good illustrator.<br /><br />Somewhere underneath all this sartorial psychedelia is a nervy, pacy, metaphysical horror story which I thought was hugely enjoyable. Set in yet another future vision of America, where the dead can be kept responsive for months in a ‘half-life’, and where there is a delicate balance between those with telekinesis and those with ‘antipsionic’ abilities, it sets up a Truman-Show-style nest of paranoia where – as often with Dick – you've never <i>completely</i> sure which layer is the real one, even after you've closed the book. If this doesn't turn out to be one of my favourites of his, I'll eat my apricot-colured felt hat.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_820822435" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/820822435" />
    <a title="Stuart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart"><img alt="Stuart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452041473p2/9456990.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/820822435?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stuart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stuart" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart">Stuart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679736646" href="/book/show/590036.Ubik">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=fantastic-weird">fantastic-weird</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=new-wave-sf">new-wave-sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=magic-realism-reality-as-illusion">magic-realism-reality-as-illusion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer820822435" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17657180438786964786">It took me 40 years to get around to it, but I finally dived into PKD's reality-bending novels over the last two years, and this one is excellent. UBIK is much stranger and more darkly humorous than Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep. It starts out with a very far-fetched future world set in 1992, and the plot revolves around telepaths, inertials, prudence organizations, snarky coin-operated household appliances, "cold-pac" half-life moratoriums, crazily excessive clothing styles, mysterious li</span>
  <span id="freeText17657180438786964786" style="display:none">It took me 40 years to get around to it, but I finally dived into PKD's reality-bending novels over the last two years, and this one is excellent. UBIK is much stranger and more darkly humorous than Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep. It starts out with a very far-fetched future world set in 1992, and the plot revolves around telepaths, inertials, prudence organizations, snarky coin-operated household appliances, "cold-pac" half-life moratoriums, crazily excessive clothing styles, mysterious life-enhancing spray cans, life force-sucking half-life entities, and scariest of all, 1939 Des Moines, Iowa~<br /><br />Well, there isn't much point describing the plot, since it just gets weirder and more disorienting as the story progresses, but surprisingly PDK is in full control of the story and tone despite the strange twists and confusion the protagonists suffer through. And although there are many humorous elements, overall the story is dark, philosophical, and just plain disorienting.<br /><br />I found the book impossible to put down and engrossing, and I really like the fact that SF books back in the 1950s/60s were SHORT and TO THE POINT. Forget about 1000-page door-stoppers like Neal Stephenson and George R.R. Martin's books, this book says what it has to say in just over 200 pages. I guess we can thank the word processor for the ridiculous bloat that so many modern SF/fantasy books suffer from. And don't get me started on series. So it's just really nice to read a book this short and sweet, but so full of ideas about life, death, the afterlife, paranoia, reality, and entropy, without providing any pat answers, all in a convenient, easy-to-use aerosol spray can.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17657180438786964786" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2894917317?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/315939.Ubik">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2894917317" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6639308553232782212">Brilliant and deeply unsettling. Worlds unravelling into something else.<br/>TBC... full review pending.<br/>Q:<br/>From the drawer beside the sink Joe Chip got a stainless steel knife; with it he began systematically to unscrew the bolt assembly of his apt's money-gulping door.<br/>"I'll sue you," the door said as the first screw fell out. <span id="freeTextContainer10234953539187529092"><i>This review can be found on <a href='http://amaranthinereads.blogspot.co.uk/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Amaranthine Reads</a></i>.<br/><br/><i>
  <b>I am Ubik. Before the universe was, I am. I made the suns. I made the worlds. I created the lives and the places they inhabit; I move them here, I put them there. They go as I say, then do as I tell them. I am the word and my name is never spoken, the name which no one knows. I am called Ubik, but that is not my name. I am. I shall always be.</b>

  <span id="freeText10234953539187529092" style="display:none"><i>This review can be found on <a href="http://amaranthinereads.blogspot.co.uk/" rel="nofollow noopener">Amaranthine Reads</a></i>.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>I am Ubik. Before the universe was, I am. I made the suns. I made the worlds. I created the lives and the places they inhabit; I move them here, I put them there. They go as I say, then do as I tell them. I am the word and my name is never spoken, the name which no one knows. I am called Ubik, but that is not my name. I am. I shall always be.</b>
</i><br /><br />Three stars, but also four stars, and two stars, and five stars and only one. <br /><br />I've not read much sci-fi and I just can't review something like this: I feel ill-equipt and under qualified. It was immense and ridiculous, well-written but with sometime shaky dialogue. A wonderful plot that was fascinating and ruddy complicated and mind-boggling. Not exactly brilliant characters, but not flat and uninteresting, either. <br /><br />Argh I just don't know. Once I've delved further in to sci-fi, and Dick in particular, I'll return to this.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><a href="https://www.infiniteanddarling.co.uk/blogs/infinite-variety-books" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="http://amaranthinereads.blogspot.co.uk/" rel="nofollow noopener">Reviews</a> | <a href="https://www.instagram.com/infiniteANDdarling/" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/infinitedarlin" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | <a href="https://uk.pinterest.com/infinitedarling/" rel="nofollow noopener">Pinterest</a> | <a href="https://www.infiniteanddarling.co.uk" rel="nofollow noopener">Shop</a> | <a href="https://www.etsy.com/uk/shop/infiniteANDdarling" rel="nofollow noopener">Etsy</a></span>
